        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'a71a835c-4ecb-48ae-8769-a91f23bd6071'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d23027638" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            <p>Stretchable energy-storage devices receive considerable attention due to their promising applications in future wearable technologies. However, they currently suffer from many problems, including low utility of active materials, limited multidirectional stretchability, and poor stability under stretched conditions. In addition, most proposed designs use one or more rigid components that fail to meet the stretchability requirement for the entire device. Here, an all-stretchable-component sodium-ion full battery based on graphene-modified poly(dimethylsiloxane) sponge electrodes and an elastic gel membrane is developed for the first time. The battery exhibits reasonable electrochemical performance and robust mechanical deformability; its electrochemical characteristics can be well-maintained under many different stretched conditions and after hundreds of stretching–release cycles. This novel design integrating all stretchable components provides a pathway toward the next generation of wearable energy devices in modern electronics.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma201700898-sec-0010">
         
         <p>There has been growing interest in stretchable energy-storage devices due to their special merits such as being ultraflexible, rollable, lightweight, and portable.<span><a href="#adma201700898-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201700898-bib-0001_R_d23027408e469" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Science">1</a>-<a href="#adma201700898-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201700898-bib-0007_R_d23027408e469" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Mater.">7</a></span> They have revolutionized a variety of applications including conformable health-monitoring skin sensors, wearable communication devices, roll-up displays, and implantable medical devices, thus they are being regarded as an important branch of modern electronics.<span><a href="#adma201700898-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201700898-bib-0008_R_d23027408e471" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Mater.">8</a>-<a href="#adma201700898-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201700898-bib-0011_R_d23027408e471" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Mater.">11</a></span> Unfortunately, most efforts have concentrated on research of storage devices only with flexible characteristics, which cannot accommodate the curved, soft, and elastic properties of the associated stretchable electronics.<span><a href="#adma201700898-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201700898-bib-0012_R_d23027408e473" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Nat. Nanotechnol.">12</a>-<a href="#adma201700898-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201700898-bib-0015_R_d23027408e473" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Energy Environ. Sci.">15</a></span> This limitation could be overcome if all the components within a storage device including the packaging, electrodes, and separators are capable of enduring large, repeated mechanical strain while maintaining their function. Although the need for such a stretchable energy-storage device configuration is easy to envisage, realizing proper materials of construction and their assembly into a device is very difficult to implement.</p>
         
         <p>Recently, several strategies have been successfully developed to assemble energy-storage devices that can provide the electrochemical properties of conventional rechargeable batteries but with the ability to be stretched, compressed, and distorted into arbitrary shapes. For example, Xu et al.<span><a href="#adma201700898-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201700898-bib-0016_R_d23027408e478" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Nat. Commun.">16</a></span> developed a novel stretchable lithium ion battery with a segmented arrangement of active materials which were interconnected using a “self-similar” design. Although the battery showed good stretchability, the large spacing between the tiny individual battery segments led to low utility of electrode materials and thus low energy density for the entire device. Chen et al.<span><a href="#adma201700898-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201700898-bib-0017_R_d23027408e480" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - ACS Nano">17</a></span> reported a stretchable supercapacitor using wrinkled graphene as both a current collector and electrode material. The device could be stretched up to 40% strain without a detectable performance change. Similar wavy or corrugated material configurations can also be found in stretchable conductors.<span><a href="#adma201700898-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201700898-bib-0018_R_d23027408e482" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Mater.">18</a>, <a href="#adma201700898-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201700898-bib-0019_R_d23027408e482" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Mater.">19</a></span> However, such electrodes can only be unidirectionally stretched which can restrict their application. Other significant designs, such as the fabrication of spring-like<span><a href="#adma201700898-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201700898-bib-0020_R_d23027408e484" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Mater.">20</a></span> and origami<span><a href="#adma201700898-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201700898-bib-0021_R_d23027408e486" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Nat. Commun.">21</a></span> structures have also been studied. However, often because of the rigidity of the electrodes, the device stability in the stretched state is typically limited. Therefore, stretchable devices are still in their infancy, and development of all-stretchable-component batteries with suitable electrochemical performance is highly desirable, yet challenging.</p>
         
         <p>As a core component, a reliable stretchable electrode is of key importance, which must offer a number of attractive characteristics, including significant mechanical durability and high electronic and ionic conductivity.<span><a href="#adma201700898-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201700898-bib-0022_R_d23027408e491" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Adv. Mater.">22</a></span> Very recently, Liu et al.<span><a href="#adma201700898-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201700898-bib-0023_R_d23027408e493" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Mater.">23</a></span> successfully developed stretchable LiFePO<sub>4</sub>/Li<sub>4</sub>Ti<sub>5</sub>O<sub>12</sub> electrodes using 3D interconnected porous poly(dimethylsiloxane) (PDMS) scaffolds as a substrate. The electrodes could maintain good electrochemical performance while enduring large strain deformations. Up to now, studies on stretchable energy-storage devices have mainly focused on lithium-ion batteries, while reports on sodium-ion batteries have been very limited. Especially, a high-performance energy-storage device beyond Li-ion batteries integrating all stretchable components, including electrode, separator, and battery package, has yet to be explored. In the present study, we report an approach to fabricate graphene-modified PDMS sponge electrodes with high electrical conductivity and good mechanical deformability. Furthermore, an all-stretchable-component sodium-ion full battery was developed, which utilized a stretchable VOPO<sub>4</sub> cathode and hard carbon anode. The PDMS sponge and sodium-ion-conducting gel polymer were used as the elastic substrate and separator, respectively. The battery exhibited good mechanical deformability with high electrochemical performance, including high capacity, good rate capability, and cycling stability.</p>
         
         <p><b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma201700898-fig-0001">1</a> shows the synthesis strategy for preparing the PDMS/reduced graphene oxide (rGO) sponge. A homogeneous PDMS gel precursor containing base/curing agents (Sylgard 184; Dow Corning) at a weight ratio of 10:1 was first placed in a dish. Sugar cubes were then placed on top of the mixture and subjected to moderate vacuum to ensure that the sugar cube templates were completely filled with PDMS precursors. After curing at 120 °C in a convection oven and washing in a bath sonicator, cubic PDMS sponges were obtained. It is noteworthy to point out that, once the sugar was dissolved away in DI water, the PDMS sponges floated on top of the water due to their low density and hydrophobicity, as shown in Figure S1 (Supporting Information). PDMS sponges prepared in this study had an average density of 0.352 ± 0.001 g cm<sup>−3</sup> and an average volume of 2.74 cm<sup>3</sup>, where the porosity was roughly ≈66.7%. In addition, the prepared PDMS sponge was of the open cell type having randomly sized and shaped pores (scanning electron microscopy (SEM) images of the PDMS sponge are shown in Figure S2, Supporting Information). The PDMS/GO sponges were prepared by immersing PDMS sponges in 5 mg mL<sup>−1</sup> GO/DI water solution by force and squeezed several times so the GO solution would infiltrate into the pores effectively. Subsequently, the PDMS/GO sponge was air dried in a hood and then exposed to HI vapor in a closed chamber. This reduction process with HI vapor changed GO to rGO (with an associated change from electrically insulating to conductive), which was confirmed by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy, and electrical conductivity measurements as shown in our previous report.<span><a href="#adma201700898-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201700898-bib-0024_R_d23027408e519" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">24</a></span> Finally, the PDMS/rGO sponge synthesis was completed by heating in a vacuum oven at 160 °C in order to eliminate residual HI vapor trapped inside the sponge. Figure S3 (Supporting Information) shows digital photos of the PDMS/rGO sponge with a lateral size of ≈14 mm. Cross-sectional SEM images of PDMS/rGO sponge revealed that the rGO was coated on the interior surfaces of the PDMS sponge evenly (as shown in Figure S4, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma201700898-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/3851a8d3-760a-4284-a0fc-68098903641a/adma201700898-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/3851a8d3-760a-4284-a0fc-68098903641a/adma201700898-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/b3100f4c-8525-4c4a-b7e6-35f218dc84eb/adma201700898-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/3851a8d3-760a-4284-a0fc-68098903641a/adma201700898-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201700898-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201700898" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Schematic of preparation steps of conductive PDMS/rGO sponge. While the PDMS/GO sponge (insulating) was brownish in color, once the material was reduced to rGO (conductive), the material appeared black.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>VOPO<sub>4</sub> and hard carbon have been explored as promising cathode and anode materials for sodium-ion batteries due to their outstanding electrochemical characteristics.<span><a href="#adma201700898-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201700898-bib-0025_R_d23027408e542" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Nano Lett.">25</a>-<a href="#adma201700898-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201700898-bib-0027_R_d23027408e542" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">27</a></span> Currently most of the works about carbonaceous anode materials for sodium-ion batteries are focused on hard carbon considering its merits of low cost, environmental benignity, and high natural abundance. As a representative 2D material, VOPO<sub>4</sub> has demonstrated outstanding rate capability owing to the Na-ion intercalation pseudocapacitance in the layered structure. In the present work, we integrated 2D VOPO<sub>4</sub> nanosheets and commercial hard carbon as the stretchable electrodes (based on the PDMS/rGO sponges), respectively. To produce the ultrathin 2D VOPO<sub>4</sub> nanosheets, bulk VOPO<sub>4</sub>·2H<sub>2</sub>O aggregates were prepared and then exfoliated in 2-propanol solvent as we previously reported.<span><a href="#adma201700898-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201700898-bib-0025_R_d23027408e555" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Nano Lett.">25</a>, <a href="#adma201700898-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201700898-bib-0028_R_d23027408e555" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Energy Environ. Sci.">28</a></span> The bulk VOPO<sub>4</sub>·2H<sub>2</sub>O aggregates have a square-shaped structure with lateral size about 10–20 µm and many tightly stacked layers (Figure S5, top-view SEM image of the samples, Supporting Information). Shown in Figure S6 (Supporting Information) are the ultrathin 2D VOPO<sub>4</sub> nanosheets deposited onto a SiO<sub>2</sub>/Si wafer, indicating that the products were nearly completely transformed from bulk to sheet-like structures by using the ultrasonication technique. The individual 2D VOPO<sub>4</sub> nanosheet thicknesses were typically less than 10 nm (Figure S6a, Supporting Information). Single VOPO<sub>4</sub> nanosheets from the diluted solution could be observed by drop-casting the solution on a lacey carbon grid as shown in Figure S6b (Supporting Information). Because of the ultrathin thickness of the VOPO<sub>4</sub> nanosheets, they exhibited nearly transparent character. The SEM and energy dispersive spectroscopy mapping of the PDMS/rGO sponge/VOPO<sub>4</sub> cathode (Figure S7, Supporting Information) revealed that the electrode materials including VOPO<sub>4</sub>, carbon Super P, and binder were fully and uniformly covered on the surface of the PDMS/rGO sponge. In addition, the PDMS/rGO sponge/hard carbon anode was also prepared and the detailed procedures can be found in the Experimental Section.</p>
         
         <p>The sodium-ion storage performance of the PDMS/rGO sponge/VOPO<sub>4</sub> cathode and PDMS/rGO sponge/hard carbon anode was enabled by adopting common active materials for Na half-cell construction using a sodium-ion-conducting gel polymer as both the separator and electrolyte. The current density (A g<sup>−1</sup> or mA g<sup>−1</sup>) and specific capacity (mA h g<sup>−1</sup>) were calculated on the basis of the mass of active material. Figure S8 (Supporting Information) shows an SEM image of the poly(vinylidene fluoride-<i>co</i>-hexafluoropropylene) (P(VDF-HFP)) membrane before soaked in an organic electrolyte with abundant interconnected pores 1–2 µm in diameter, which provide sufficient channels for the transport of Na ions. The membrane was used as the matrix of the electrolyte before gelling which can absorb and retain the organic electrolyte efficiently. Shown in Figure S9 (Supporting Information) are the digital photos of the P(VDF-HFP) membrane (left). When the electrolyte was successfully imbibed into the membrane, it appeared transparent (Figure S9 (right), Supporting Information). In addition, owing to the linking of the HFP unit in the polymer chain, P(VDF-HFP) exhibits a significantly reduced degree of crystallinity with superior flexibility, thus the shape and size of the P(VDF-HFP) membrane can be freely changed in a manner suitable for many flexible electronics. The galvanostatic charge–discharge curves of the PDMS/rGO sponge/VOPO<sub>4</sub> electrode at different current densities, in the potential window between 2.5 and 4.3 V (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>), are presented in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma201700898-fig-0002">2</a>a. Obviously, the PDMS/rGO sponge/VOPO<sub>4</sub> electrode exhibits two main charge–discharge plateaus, which agree with its cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves as shown in Figure S10a (Supporting Information). The oxidation peak at 3.75 V and reduction peak at 3.4 V correspond to the redox couples of V<sup>5+</sup>/V<sup>4+</sup>. Figure S10b (Supporting Information) shows the first charge–discharge of the VOPO<sub>4</sub> at a current density of 50 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, and it can be found that the initial discharge capacity of the VOPO<sub>4</sub> electrode was relatively low; however, the Coulombic efficiency was nearly 100% in the subsequent cycles. Similar phenomena can also be found in other recently reported work, and the initial capacity loss can be offset by adding presodiation agent.<span><a href="#adma201700898-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201700898-bib-0029_R_d23027408e612" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Chem. Mater.">29</a>-<a href="#adma201700898-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201700898-bib-0032_R_d23027408e612" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">32</a></span> The electrochemical characteristics of various current-density tests for the VOPO<sub>4</sub> are shown in Figure <a href="#adma201700898-fig-0002">2</a>b and Figure S10c (Supporting Information). Clearly, the PDMS/rGO sponge/VOPO<sub>4</sub> displays superior rate capability and good cycling stability. A stable capacity of ≈126 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> can be achieved at the current density of 50 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. It can still deliver a high capacity of ≈83 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> even at a high current density of 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>. The high rate capability can mainly be contributed to three factors. (1) The fast kinetics of pseudocapacitive intercalation in 2D VOPO<sub>4</sub> nanosheets. (2) The high ionic conductivity of the highly porous semicrystalline P(VDF-HFP) membrane. (3) The hierarchical PDMS substrate with uniform rGO coating to form a 3D conductive network in the bridged structure. Figure <a href="#adma201700898-fig-0002">2</a>c shows the cycling performance of the PDMS/rGO sponge/VOPO<sub>4</sub> at a current density of 0.4 A g<sup>−1</sup>. It displays a remarkable reversible capacity retention of ≈85% after 300 cycles, corresponding to ≈0.05% capacity fading per cycle.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma201700898-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/1f94e8bf-98d5-4d01-9003-59cd1ba163fb/adma201700898-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/1f94e8bf-98d5-4d01-9003-59cd1ba163fb/adma201700898-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/9211e280-946e-4218-8311-fbb04e962458/adma201700898-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/1f94e8bf-98d5-4d01-9003-59cd1ba163fb/adma201700898-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201700898-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201700898" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical characteristics of PDMS/rGO sponge/VOPO<sub>4</sub> and PDMS/rGO sponge/hard carbon electrode in half cells. a) Representative charge–discharge curves of the PDMS/rGO sponge/VOPO<sub>4</sub> between the voltage range of 2.5–4.3 V at different current densities. b,c) The corresponding rate capability (b) and cycling performance (c) with Coulombic efficiency over 300 cycles at a current density of 0.4 A g<sup>−1</sup>. d) The typical charge–discharge profiles of the PDMS/rGO sponge/hard carbon between 0.01 and 2.5 V at various current densities. e,f) The corresponding rate capability (e) and cycling performance (f) with Coulombic efficiency over 300 cycles at the current density of 0.4 A g<sup>−1</sup>.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Shown in Figure S11a (Supporting Information) are the representative CVs of the initial five cycles of the PDMS/rGO sponge/hard carbon at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup> between 0.01 and 2.0 V (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>). In the first cathodic scan, a wide irreversible peak at ≈0.48 V is observed, which can be ascribed to the formation of a solid–electrolyte interface film.<span><a href="#adma201700898-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201700898-bib-0033_R_d23027408e669" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Energy Environ. Sci.">33</a>, <a href="#adma201700898-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201700898-bib-0034_R_d23027408e669" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - ACS Nano">34</a></span> Figure S11b (Supporting Information) shows the first, second, and third charge–discharge profiles of the PDMS/rGO sponge/hard carbon electrode at a current density of 50 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. The irreversible capacity loss that occurred in the first discharge curves is consistent with the above CV analysis. Figure <a href="#adma201700898-fig-0002">2</a>d presents the charge–discharge curves at various current densities ranging from 50 mA g<sup>−1</sup> to 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. The discharge slopes of the PDMS/rGO sponge/hard carbon gradually shift toward lower potentials, which is due to the increased cell polarization at high current densities.<span><a href="#adma201700898-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201700898-bib-0035_R_d23027408e680" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Chem. Mater.">35</a>, <a href="#adma201700898-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201700898-bib-0036_R_d23027408e680" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Small">36</a></span> The rate capability of the PDMS/rGO sponge/hard carbon is shown in Figure <a href="#adma201700898-fig-0002">2</a>e and Figure S11c (Supporting Information). When the current density gradually increased from 0.05 to 0.1, 0.2, 0.4, 0.6, 0.8, and 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the corresponding average discharge capacities decreased from 188 to 135, 94, 72, 62, 56, and 54 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. These results demonstrate the good electrochemical kinetics and sodium-storage properties of the PDMS/rGO sponge/hard carbon. Moreover, the capacity of the PDMS/rGO sponge/hard carbon on cycling at current density of 0.4 A g<sup>−1</sup> is shown in Figure <a href="#adma201700898-fig-0002">2</a>f. It is found that the capacity retention shows nearly no decay over the voltage range from 0.01 V to 2.0 V after 300 cycles, indicating the outstanding stability of the PDMS/rGO sponge/hard carbon electrode. The appealing sodium-ion storage characteristic and high stability of both the PDMS/rGO sponge/VOPO<sub>4</sub> cathode and PDMS/rGO sponge/hard carbon anode are beneficial to the improvement of the electrochemical performance when they are assembled in the sodium-ion full batteries.</p>
         
         <p>Following the electrochemical characterization of the PDMS/rGO sponge/VOPO<sub>4</sub> and PDMS/rGO sponge/hard carbon electrodes, we built the stretchable PDMS/rGO sponge/VOPO<sub>4</sub>//PDMS/rGO sponge/hard carbon full battery, which is shown in the inset image of <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma201700898-fig-0003">3</a>d. The VOPO<sub>4</sub> cathode and hard carbon anode were first laminated onto both sides of the square-shaped P(VDF-HFP) based sodium-ion-conducting gel polymer, and then sealed with the PDMS substrate in an Ar-filled glove box. The mechanical properties of the fabricated stretchable sodium-ion full battery were evaluated by tensile strain measurements as shown in Figure S12 (Supporting Information). It can be seen clearly that the full battery exhibited excellent stretchability up to 60%. However, for the VOPO<sub>4</sub> cathode and hard carbon anode, they can be stretched beyond 75%. The electrochemical performance of the stretchable sodium-ion full battery was first performed without any strain applied. At 0.1 C, a high reversible capacity of ≈103 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> was achieved as shown in Figure <a href="#adma201700898-fig-0003">3</a>a. It can be maintained at 96 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> and 92 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> while stretched at 20% and 50% strains, respectively (photographs of the stretched battery are shown in Figure <a href="#adma201700898-fig-0003">3</a>e,f). After 100 cycles of stretching to 50% strain, the full battery retained over 89% of specific capacity (Figure <a href="#adma201700898-fig-0003">3</a>c). The excellent mechanical stability can also be corroborated from SEM images of both the electrode material and the gel separator before and after stretches, as presented in Figure S13–S15 (Supporting Information), and no obvious variations can be found after extensive stretches. It should be noted that no delamination was found for the electrode material. This was possible because all of the conductive materials were not only physically attached firmly to the substrate, but also encapsulated in a highly porous sponge.<span><a href="#adma201700898-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201700898-bib-0037_R_d23027408e725" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Appl. Phys. Lett.">37</a>, <a href="#adma201700898-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201700898-bib-0038_R_d23027408e725" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">38</a></span> It can be expected that introducing favorable interactions by functionalization of graphene may further improve long-term stability of the electrode. The stretchable sodium-ion full battery was also tested at various C rates as shown in Figure <a href="#adma201700898-fig-0003">3</a>b. The discharge capacity was 88 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.2 C, and even at the rate of 1.0 C, the reversible capacity was still 53 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>. Figure <a href="#adma201700898-fig-0003">3</a>d is the cycling stability of the stretchable sodium-ion full battery showing that it can maintain ≈85% capacity retention after 100 cycles of sodium-ion extraction and insertion at the rate of 1.0 C. Different from half battery in which the source of Na ion is well abundant, the less stable cyclability of full battery might be caused by the minor irreversibility considering not 100% Coulombic efficiency. Generally speaking, the good electrochemical performance of this stretchable sodium-ion full battery was mainly attributed to the PDMS/rGO sponge based electrodes, which not only offer remarkable mechanical robustness but also provide a hierarchical 3D open porous and high stable architecture for fast sodium-ion transport and storage.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma201700898-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/9c4d7592-3929-41f4-90a4-bca6e288b8ef/adma201700898-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/9c4d7592-3929-41f4-90a4-bca6e288b8ef/adma201700898-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/2c9b5f0d-1aa9-4999-b241-76c6ef778a30/adma201700898-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/9c4d7592-3929-41f4-90a4-bca6e288b8ef/adma201700898-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201700898-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201700898" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance of the stretchable PDMS/rGO sponge/VOPO4//PDMS/rGO sponge/hard carbon sodium-ion full battery. a) The charge–discharge curves of the full battery in the unstretched and stretched states with different strains (a rate of 1.0 C corresponds to 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, the specific capacity is calculated based on the mass of the cathode materials). b) Rate capability of the full battery at various C rates. c) Charge–discharge curves of the full battery after 50 and 100 stretching–release cycles at 0.1 C. d) Cycling stability of the full battery at the rate of 1.0 C.                </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma201700898-fig-0004">4</a>a is a schematic illustration of the manufactured stretchable PDMS/rGO sponge/VOPO<sub>4</sub>//PDMS/rGO sponge/hard carbon sodium-ion full battery. The detailed preparation process can be found in the Experimental Section. Such an all-stretchable-component sodium-ion full battery with remarkable mechanical robustness is promising. The batteries and their fabrication processes are cost-effective, potentially scalable, and they can be compatible with facile manufacturing processes, through which larger stretchable rechargeable full batteries can be obtained. Figure <a href="#adma201700898-fig-0004">4</a>b shows that the presented stretchable sodium-ion full battery is able to power a commercial blue LED light in the stretched state with ≈50% strains. Furthermore, we even created a stretchable sodium-ion full battery mounted on an athletic elbow brace that can curve significantly around the elbow as shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma201700898-fig-0005">5</a>. Importantly, the full battery can work normally during stretching to adapt to various elbow movements without destroying the battery structures.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma201700898-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a279f8cf-cc97-42fe-96c0-3b68248ce211/adma201700898-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/a279f8cf-cc97-42fe-96c0-3b68248ce211/adma201700898-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/6e8bbef2-d8ab-47e4-922a-98551f1dcd15/adma201700898-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a279f8cf-cc97-42fe-96c0-3b68248ce211/adma201700898-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201700898-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201700898" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Schematic illustration of the fabricated stretchable PDMS/rGO sponge/VOPO<sub>4</sub>//PDMS/rGO sponge/hard carbon sodium-ion full battery. b) Photographs of the stretchable sodium-ion full battery in the unstretched state (top) and stretched state with ≈50% strains (bottom) to power a commercial LED light.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma201700898-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c221f673-2533-4786-89ce-1e58b9cf5ab6/adma201700898-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/c221f673-2533-4786-89ce-1e58b9cf5ab6/adma201700898-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/1aed5fc1-cfe4-45c9-96df-437579372230/adma201700898-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c221f673-2533-4786-89ce-1e58b9cf5ab6/adma201700898-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201700898-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201700898" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Photographs of the stretchable sodium-ion full battery mounted on an athletic elbow brace under different bending states where it continuously powers a commercial LED light.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>In summary, we have shown that an all-stretchable-component sodium-ion full battery can be fabricated by employing the PDMS/rGO sponge/VOPO<sub>4</sub> cathode, P(VDF-HFP) gel separator/electrolyte, and PDMS/rGO sponge/hard carbon anode. The battery exhibited good electrochemical and mechanical stability with highly reversible capacity, even under harshly stretched conditions and after hundreds of stretching–release cycles. These characteristics can be ascribed to the tailored properties of the PDMS/rGO sponge based electrodes, which includes high electrochemical conductivity, stable porous architecture, as well as the robust mechanical deformability. Given that both the electrode substrate and battery package are made of PDMS, the strain limit is governed by the intrinsic material properties of PDMS elastomer.<span><a href="#adma201700898-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201700898-bib-0039_R_d23027408e810" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Sci. Rep.">39</a></span> To improve the stretchability beyond these limits, several approaches have been proposed, including exploiting wrinkled PDMS substrates with prestretching, or developing 3D nanoarchitectured elastomer.<span><a href="#adma201700898-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201700898-bib-0039_R_d23027408e812" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Sci. Rep.">39</a>, <a href="#adma201700898-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201700898-bib-0040_R_d23027408e812" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Nat. Commun.">40</a></span> It is also critical to explore the in-depth degradation mechanism to extend the lifetime of our stretchable battery in the near future research. Additionally, the electrode materials can also be extended to other electroactive materials, and this PDMS/rGO sponge design offers a novel platform for stretchable energy-storage devices, energy conversion/harvesting devices, or even electronic devices. Last but not least, larger sized stretchable sodium-ion full batteries could also be prepared from scalable manufacturing processes, which is promising for further development of stretchable electronics with high-performance that could be applied in bendable, wearable, and stretchable electronic devices.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma201700898-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma201700898-sec-0020-title"> Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <p><i>Materials</i>: PDMS elastomer prepolymer (Sylgard 184, Dow Corning) was purchased and used without any further purification. Preoxidized graphite from Bay Carbon Inc. (SP-1) was used in order to synthesize graphene oxide (GO) and sugar cubes were purchased from local supermarket. GO was synthesized according to the Hummer's method with slight modification as described elsewhere.<span><a href="#adma201700898-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201700898-bib-0024_R_d23027408e824" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">24</a></span> A 55% hydriodic acid (HI) solution was used as the reducing agent for GO and was purchased from Sigma–Aldrich. All other reagents, such as sulfuric acid, potassium permanganate, hydrogen peroxide, and hydrochloric acid to prepare GO, were purchased from Fisher Scientific and used as-received.</p>
         
         <p><i>Preparation of PDMS Sponges</i>: Highly porous PDMS/GO sponges were synthesized as previously demonstrated by Choi and co-workers<span><a href="#adma201700898-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201700898-bib-0041_R_d23027408e830" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">41</a></span> by preparing a mixture of PDMS precursor and thermal curing agent in a ratio of 10:1 by weight. The mixture was thoroughly mixed using a wooden spatula in a plastic Petri dish for several minutes. After degassing the PDMS mixture in a vacuum oven for 1 h at room temperature, sugar cube templates were placed on top of the mixture and were subjected to moderate vacuum for additional 4 h. Once the sugar cube template was completely filled with PDMS, excess amount of PDMS was wiped gently using a Kimwipe and then cured at 120 °C for 2 h in a convection oven. Finally, sugars were washed away by putting the samples in a bath sonicator filled with DI water. Typically, bath sonication was performed at 40 °C for 4 h while changing the bath with fresh DI water every 30 min.</p>
         
         <p><i>Preparation of PDMS/rGO Sponges</i>: Electrically conductive PDMS/rGO sponges were prepared by immersing PDMS sponges in 5 mg mL<sup>−1</sup> GO/DI water solution, unless noted differently. GO solution was prepared by mechanical stirring at room temperature followed by probe sonication (Branson Digital Sonifier 450) for 10 min in an ice bath (total energy 24 kJ). Due to the hydrophobicity of the PDMS sponge, the material was immersed in the solution by force and squeezed several times so the GO solution can infiltrate into the pores effectively. After PDMS/GO sponge was prepared, the material was dried in the hood for 1 d and then exposed to HI vapor in a closed chamber for additional 1 d. Finally, in order to eliminate residual HI vapor trapped inside the sponge, the final product was heated at 160 °C for 24 h in a vacuum oven.</p>
         
         <p><i>Synthesis of Sodium-Ion-Conducting Gel Polymer</i>: The preparation of the P(VDF-HFP) membrane was based on the literature reported by Wu's group with minor modifications.<span><a href="#adma201700898-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201700898-bib-0042_R_d23027408e842" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Solid State Ionics">42</a></span> Typically, P(VDF-HFP) was dissolved into a mixture of DMF and distilled water at 80 °C with a weight ratio of P(VDF-HFP): DMF: H<sub>2</sub>O = 15:85:3. The solution was cast onto a clean glass plate, and then immersed in a water bath at 80 °C, yielding a homogeneous white membrane. Then the white membrane was dried under vacuum at 100 °C for 10 h. After that, the dried membrane was punched into circular pieces. For the all-stretchable-component sodium-ion full batteries, the membrane was cut according to the size of the stretchable anode or cathode. Finally, these membrane were soaked in an organic electrolyte (1 mol L<sup>−1</sup> NaClO<sub>4</sub> in propylene carbonate with 2 vol% fluorinated ethylene carbonate as an electrolyte additive) over 12 h in a glove box (water content: &lt;1 ppm) to obtain the gel-polymer electrolyte.</p>
         
         <p><i>Synthesis of VOPO<sub>4</sub> Nanosheets</i>: Bulk VOPO<sub>4</sub>·2H<sub>2</sub>O chunks were synthesized according to previous literature.<span><a href="#adma201700898-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201700898-bib-0026_R_d23027408e862" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Nat. Commun.">26</a>, <a href="#adma201700898-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201700898-bib-0043_R_d23027408e862" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Aust. J. Chem.">43</a></span> Briefly, a mixture of V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> (4.8 g), H<sub>3</sub>PO<sub>4</sub> (85%, 26.6 mL), and H<sub>2</sub>O (115.4 mL) was refluxed at 110 °C for 16 h. The resulting yellow precipitate was collected by centrifugation, washed several times with water and acetone, and then dried in oven at 80 °C. To obtain exfoliated VOPO<sub>4</sub> nanosheets, bulk VOPO<sub>4</sub>·2H<sub>2</sub>O chunks were first ground to ≈1 µm in size then dispersed in 2-propanol with concentration about 2 mg mL<sup>−1</sup>. The yellow dispersion was ultrasonicated in water for 30 min, during which color of the dispersion became faded, indicating successful formation of VOPO<sub>4</sub> nanosheets.</p>
         
         <p><i>Characterizations</i>: SEM, scanning transmission electron microscopy (STEM), and transmission electron microscopy (TEM) observations were carried out on a Hitachi scanning electron microscope (S-5500) and a JEOL transmission electron microscope (2010F), respectively. All the mechanical tests were conducted on an Instron 5966 with wedge action grips. Both edges of the samples were clamped by grips, and the displacement and load could be recorded using Bluehill software. In battery test, the edges of the package could be adhered to fixed binder clips to apply the strain. Strain was the change in length divided by the original length, and the images of original battery and extended battery are shown in the insets of Figure <a href="#adma201700898-fig-0003">3</a>d–f, separately.</p>
         
         <p><i>Electrochemistry</i>: Half cells were constructed using the CR2032 coin-type cells with Na metal as the counter and reference electrode, and sodium-ion-conducting gel polymer as the electrolyte. The PDMS/rGO sponge was sectioned into disks with area of 1 cm<sup>2</sup> and with thickness of ≈800 µm. The thin disk which acted as the stretchable electrode substrate was treated using the UV-ozone cleaner (Model, No. 42, Jelight Company Inc., Irvine, CA, USA). The VOPO<sub>4</sub> nanosheet cathode was prepared by adding Super P carbon and sodium carboxymethyl cellulose (CMC) directly into aforementioned 2-propanol dispersion after ultrasonication (weight ratio of VOPO<sub>4</sub>:Super P:CMC = 80:15:5). While heated to remove excess 2-propanol, the mixture was thoroughly mixed in a Thinky centrifugal mixer to form a homogenous slurry, which was then cast on the thin PDMS/rGO disks. The anode was also prepared by a slurry-coating procedure. The slurry consisted of 90 wt% Kureha hard carbon, 5 wt% Super P, and 5 wt% poly(vinylidene difluoride) dissolved in <i>N</i>-methylpyrrolidone was also cast on the above thin PDMS/rGO disks. For the stretchable sodium-ion full battery, the square-shaped PDMS/rGO sponge with the thickness of ≈800 µm was used as the electrode substrate. The electrodes's preparation process was the same to the disk-shaped cathode and anode of the half cells. The mass loading of the cathode was ≈6 mg cm<sup>−2</sup>. Electrochemical measurements of the cells were conducted using two-electrode cells at room temperature. CV curves were collected by Bio-logic potentiostat (VMP3) at 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup>, Galvanostatic charge/discharge experiments were performed at different current densities on LAND battery cycler (CT2001A).</p>
         
         <p>PDMS elastic substrate was prepared by mixing base/curing agents (Sylgard 184; Dow Corning) (10:1, mass/mass), followed by degassing in a vacuum oven for 40 min and thermally curing at 80 °C for 1 h. Then the stretchable VOPO<sub>4</sub> and hard carbon electrodes were laminated onto both sides of the sodium-ion-conducting gel polymer and sealed with PDMS. Prior to full cells fabrication, the presodiation of VOPO<sub>4</sub> cathode and hard carbon anode was performed to carry out the first cycle activation. The cell balance was achieved by setting the electrode mass ratio of cathode/anode to ≈1.25.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
